			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">2007–08 Writers Guild of America strike</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%932008_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 Writers Guild of America strike">2007–2008 Writers Guild of America strike</a>)</span></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:241px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Writers_raise_signs_at_wga_rally.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Writers_raise_signs_at_wga_rally.jpg/239px-Writers_raise_signs_at_wga_rally.jpg" width="239" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Writers_raise_signs_at_wga_rally.jpg/359px-Writers_raise_signs_at_wga_rally.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Writers_raise_signs_at_wga_rally.jpg/478px-Writers_raise_signs_at_wga_rally.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="750" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Writers_raise_signs_at_wga_rally.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Striking writers and supporters raise signs at a WGAW rally in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>2007–08 Writers Guild of America strike</b>, more commonly referred to at the time as simply the <b>Writers' Strike</b>, was a <a href="/wiki/Strike_action" title="Strike action">strike</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Writers_Guild_of_America,_East" title="Writers Guild of America, East">Writers Guild of America, East</a> (WGAE) and the <a href="/wiki/Writers_Guild_of_America,_West" title="Writers Guild of America, West">Writers Guild of America, West</a> (WGAW).<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_07-11-02_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_07-11-02-1">[1]</a></sup> The WGAE and WGAW <a href="/wiki/Labor_union" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor union">labor unions</a> represent film, television, and radio writers working in the United States. All 12,000 screenwriters and TV writers in the guild were part of the strike<sup id="cite_ref-slt-110407_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slt-110407-2">[2]</a></sup> which started on November 5, 2007, and concluded on February 12, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The strike sought increased funding for the writers in comparison to the profits of the larger studios. It was targeted at the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Motion_Picture_and_Television_Producers" title="Alliance of Motion Picture and Television Producers">Alliance of Motion Picture and Television Producers</a> (AMPTP), a <a href="/wiki/Trade_organization" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade organization">trade organization</a> representing the interests of 397 American film and television producers.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The most influential of these are eleven corporations: <a href="/wiki/CBS_Corporation" title="CBS Corporation">CBS</a> (headed by <a href="/wiki/Les_Moonves" class="mw-redirect" title="Les Moonves">Les Moonves</a>), <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">MGM</a> (<a href="/wiki/Harry_E._Sloan" title="Harry E. Sloan">Harry E. Sloan</a>), <a href="/wiki/NBCUniversal" title="NBCUniversal">NBCUniversal</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Zucker" class="mw-redirect" title="Jeffrey Zucker">Jeffrey Zucker</a>), <a href="/wiki/The_Weinstein_Company" title="The Weinstein Company">The Weinstein Company</a> (<a href="/wiki/Harvey_Weinstein" title="Harvey Weinstein">Harvey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bob_Weinstein" title="Bob Weinstein">Bob Weinstein</a>), <a href="/wiki/Lionsgate" title="Lionsgate">Lionsgate</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jon_Feltheimer" title="Jon Feltheimer">Jon Feltheimer</a>), <a href="/wiki/News_Corp" title="News Corp">News Corp</a>/<a href="/wiki/Fox_Entertainment_Group" title="Fox Entertainment Group">Fox</a> (<a href="/wiki/Peter_Chernin" title="Peter Chernin">Peter Chernin</a>), <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a> (<a href="/wiki/Brad_Grey" title="Brad Grey">Brad Grey</a>), <a href="/wiki/Anchor_Bay_Entertainment" title="Anchor Bay Entertainment">Anchor Bay</a>/<a href="/wiki/Liberty_Media" title="Liberty Media">Liberty Media</a>/<a href="/wiki/Starz_(TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Starz (TV channel)">Starz</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chris_McGurk" title="Chris McGurk">Chris McGurk</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures_Entertainment" class="mw-redirect" title="Sony Pictures Entertainment">Sony Pictures</a> (<a href="/wiki/Michael_Lynton" title="Michael Lynton">Michael Lynton</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Walt Disney Company">Walt Disney Company</a> (<a href="/wiki/Robert_Iger" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Iger">Robert Iger</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> (<a href="/wiki/Barry_Meyer" title="Barry Meyer">Barry Meyer</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Negotiators for the striking writers reached a tentative agreement on February 8, 2008, and the boards of both guilds unanimously approved the deal on February 10, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-variety_08-02-10_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-variety_08-02-10-7">[7]</a></sup> Striking writers voted on February 12, 2008 on whether to lift the restraining order, with 92.5% voting to end the strike.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> On February 26, the WGA announced that the contract had been ratified with a 93.6% approval among WGA members.<sup id="cite_ref-wga_08-02-26_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wga_08-02-26-9">[9]</a></sup> The Writers Guild later requested a court order seeking that the agreement be honored and implemented.</p>
<p>The guilds were on strike for 14 weeks and 2 days (100 days).<sup id="cite_ref-100days_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100days-10">[10]</a></sup> In contrast, the previous <a href="/wiki/1988_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike" title="1988 Writers Guild of America strike">strike in 1988</a>, the longest in the history of the Guild, lasted 21 weeks and 6 days (153 days), costing the American entertainment industry an estimated $500 million in <a href="/wiki/Opportunity_cost" title="Opportunity cost">opportunity costs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> According to a <a href="/wiki/National_Public_Radio" class="mw-redirect" title="National Public Radio">National Public Radio</a> (NPR) report filed on February 12, 2008, the strike cost the economy of <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> an estimated $1.5 billion. A report from the <a href="/wiki/UCLA_Anderson_School_of_Management" title="UCLA Anderson School of Management">UCLA Anderson School of Management</a> put the loss at $380 million, while economist Jack Kyser put the loss at $2.1 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The resolution of the strike was unclear: while they lost out on short-term deals, they received a new percentage payment on the distributor’s gross for digital distribution based on the deal that the DGA made during the strike.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Issues_in_the_strike">Issues in the strike</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Issues in the strike">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jeff_garlin_and_others_at_wga_rally.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Jeff_garlin_and_others_at_wga_rally.jpg/220px-Jeff_garlin_and_others_at_wga_rally.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Jeff_garlin_and_others_at_wga_rally.jpg/330px-Jeff_garlin_and_others_at_wga_rally.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Jeff_garlin_and_others_at_wga_rally.jpg/440px-Jeff_garlin_and_others_at_wga_rally.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="750" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jeff_garlin_and_others_at_wga_rally.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Writer-actor <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Garlin" title="Jeff Garlin">Jeff Garlin</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Curb_Your_Enthusiasm" title="Curb Your Enthusiasm">Curb Your Enthusiasm</a></i> (foreground, right) and others at a WGAW rally outside the <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">Fox Studios</a> in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>We are ready to meet at any time and remain committed to reaching a fair and reasonable deal that keeps the industry working, but the DVD issue is a roadblock to these negotiations.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">AMPTP president Nick Counter<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></cite></div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>Every issue that matters to writers, including Internet reuse, original writing for new media, DVDs, and jurisdiction, has been ignored. This is completely unacceptable.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">WGA Negotiating Committee<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Every three years, the Writers Guild negotiates a new basic contract with the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Motion_Picture_and_Television_Producers" title="Alliance of Motion Picture and Television Producers">AMPTP</a> by which its members are employed. This contract is called the Minimum Basic Agreement (MBA).<sup id="cite_ref-04mba_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-04mba-18">[18]</a></sup> In 2007, negotiations over the MBA reached an <a href="/wiki/Impasse" title="Impasse">impasse</a> and the WGA membership voted to give its board authorization to call a strike, which it did on Friday, November 2, 2007; the strike began the following Monday, November 5, 2007.</p>
<p>Among the many proposals from both sides regarding the new contract, there were several key issues of contention including DVD residuals, union jurisdiction over <a href="/wiki/Animation" title="Animation">animation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reality_show" class="mw-redirect" title="Reality show">reality program</a> writers, and compensation for "<a href="/wiki/New_media" title="New media">new media</a>" (content written for or distributed through emerging digital technology such as the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DVD_residuals">DVD residuals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: DVD residuals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1985 the Writers Guild went on strike over the <a href="/wiki/Home_video" title="Home video">home video</a> market, which was then small and primarily consisted of distribution via video tape. At that time, the entertainment companies argued home video was an "unproven" market, with an expensive delivery channel (manufacturing <a href="/wiki/VHS" title="VHS">VHS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Betamax" title="Betamax">Betamax</a> tapes, and to a much smaller extent, <a href="/wiki/LaserDisc" title="LaserDisc">LaserDisc</a>). Movies were selling in the range of between $40–$100 per tape, and the Guild accepted a formula in which a writer would receive a small percentage (0.3%) of the first million of reportable gross (and 0.36% after) of each tape sold as a <a href="/wiki/Residual_(entertainment_industry)" title="Residual (entertainment industry)">residual</a>. As manufacturing costs for video tapes dropped dramatically and the home video market exploded, writers came to feel they had been shortchanged by this deal.<sup id="cite_ref-latimes_strike_authorized_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latimes_strike_authorized-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVDs</a> debuted in 1996 and rapidly replaced the more-expensive VHS format, outselling VHS for the first time on the week of June 15, 2003. The previous VHS residual formula continued to apply to DVDs.</p>
<p>Prior to the strike, the home video market had become the major source of revenue for the movie studios. In April 2004, the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> reported the companies made $4.8 billion in home video sales versus $1.78 billion at the <a href="/wiki/Box_office" title="Box office">box office</a> between January and March.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposals">Proposals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Proposals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>WGA members argued that a writer's residuals are a necessary part of a writer's income that is typically relied upon during periods of unemployment common in the writing industry. The WGA requested a doubling of the residual rate for DVD sales, which would result in a residual of 0.6% (up from 0.3%) per DVD sold.<sup id="cite_ref-wgaproposal_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wgaproposal-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Motion_Picture_and_Television_Producers" title="Alliance of Motion Picture and Television Producers">AMPTP</a> maintained that studios' DVD income was necessary to offset rising production and marketing costs.<sup id="cite_ref-latimes_strike_authorized_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latimes_strike_authorized-20">[20]</a></sup> They further insisted that the current DVD formula (0.3%) be applied to residuals in other digital media—an area which was also contested by the Writers Guild.</p>
<p>The WGA provisionally removed the increased DVD residual request from the table, in an effort to avert a strike and on the understanding of certain concessions by the AMPTP, the night before the strike began. However, after the strike began, WGAW President <a href="/wiki/Patric_M._Verrone" class="mw-redirect" title="Patric M. Verrone">Patric M. Verrone</a> wrote that the membership exhibited "significant disappointment and even anger" when they learned of the proposed removal of the request; and Verrone also wrote that, since the removal of the increased DVD residual request was contingent on concessions by the AMPTP which did not happen, the writers would and should continue to "fight to get our fair share of the residuals of the future."<sup id="cite_ref-dvd_proposal_removal_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dvd_proposal_removal-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conclusion">Conclusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Conclusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There was no change to the calculation of DVD residuals.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_media">New media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: New media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Patric_m_verrone_at_wga_rally.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Patric_m_verrone_at_wga_rally.jpg/220px-Patric_m_verrone_at_wga_rally.jpg" width="220" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Patric_m_verrone_at_wga_rally.jpg/330px-Patric_m_verrone_at_wga_rally.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Patric_m_verrone_at_wga_rally.jpg/440px-Patric_m_verrone_at_wga_rally.jpg 2x" data-file-width="712" data-file-height="707" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Patric_m_verrone_at_wga_rally.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
WGA West president and <i><a href="/wiki/Futurama" title="Futurama">Futurama</a></i> writer <a href="/wiki/Patric_Verrone" title="Patric Verrone">Patric Verrone</a> speaks at a strike rally in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Driven by the then-recent contract between <a href="/wiki/Viacom" title="Viacom">Viacom</a> and the creators of <i><a href="/wiki/South_Park" title="South Park">South Park</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> one of the critical issues for the negotiations was residuals for "<a href="/wiki/New_media" title="New media">new media</a>", or compensation for delivery channels such as Internet downloads, <a href="/wiki/IPTV" title="IPTV">IPTV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Streaming_media" title="Streaming media">streaming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smart_phone" class="mw-redirect" title="Smart phone">smart phone</a> programming, straight-to-Internet content, and other "on-demand" online distribution methods, along with <a href="/wiki/Video_on_demand" title="Video on demand">video on demand</a> on cable and satellite television.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_2">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Prior to the strike, the WGA had no arrangement with the companies regarding the use of content online, and two models of Internet distribution are currently being negotiated. The first is "electronic sell-through" (also known as "Internet sales" or "digital sell-through"). In electronic sell-through, the consumer purchases a copy of the program and downloads it to a <a href="/wiki/Computer_data_storage" title="Computer data storage">local storage device</a> for subsequent viewing at their convenience. Examples include movies and television shows purchased through the <a href="/wiki/ITunes_Store" title="ITunes Store">iTunes Store</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Video_on_Demand" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon Video on Demand">Amazon Video on Demand</a>. In the second model, "<a href="/wiki/Streaming_media" title="Streaming media">streaming</a> video", the consumer watches a program in real time as it is transmitted to their computer but is usually not saved. Current examples of this model include advertising-supported television programs streamed free to the audience, such as those available at nbc.com, abc.com, fox.com, cbs.com, thedailyshow.com, and hulu.com.</p>
<p>In either case, the program may be viewed directly on a computer or on a traditional television via media distribution devices (<i>e.g.</i> <a href="/wiki/TiVo" title="TiVo">TiVo</a>). The convenience of both these technologies lowers the barriers to entry into the digital distribution marketplace making it more accessible to mainstream consumers.</p>
<p>It was widely expected by industry observers that new media would eventually supplant both DVD in the home video market and television in the broadcasting market as the primary means for distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> As in the mid-1980s, the companies argued that new media represents an unproven and untested market and asked for additional time for study. However, feeling resentment from the 20-year-old home video deal and unwilling to make similar concessions in a so-called "new market" yet again, WGA members remained adamant that whatever deal they made for new media, it could not resemble the DVD formula.</p>
<p>New media was widely seen by most WGA writers as the central issue for the strike. Writer-director <a href="/wiki/Craig_Mazin" title="Craig Mazin">Craig Mazin</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Scary_Movie_3" title="Scary Movie 3">Scary Movie 3</a></i>) has dubbed new media "the One Issue" that matters.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>This sentiment was further articulated by a self-described "skeptic", writer Howard Gould, at a meeting of the full WGA membership the night before the strike date was announced. He said, to a standing ovation:</p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">Soon, when computers and your TV are connected, that's how we're all going to watch. Okay? Those residuals are going to go from what they are towards zero if we don't make a stand now. ... This is such a big issue that if they see us roll over on this without making a stand - three years from now, they're gonna be back for something else. ... I might have been the most moderate one up here when we started, but I sat there in the room the first day and they read us those thirty-two pages of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/rollback" class="extiw" title="wikt:rollback">rollbacks</a>. And what they wanted us to hear was that "if you don't give us what [we] want on the important thing, we're gonna come after you for all those other things." But what I heard was, if we give them that thing, they'll <i>still</i> come after us for those other things. And in three years, it'll be "we want to revamp the whole residual system," and in another three years, it'll be "y'know what, we don't really want to fund the health fund the way we've been." And then it will be pension. And then it'll be credit determination. And there just is that time when everybody has to see—this is one where we just gotta stand our ground.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conclusion_2">Conclusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Conclusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Despite the strike, the WGA "didn't get anything close to what they wanted" in New Media.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Instead, the WGA took the DGA's deal: For downloads, writers were granted 1.2% of distributor’s gross receipts for rentals and 0.65%-0.7% of gross receipts. For ad supported streaming writers were granted 2% of gross receipts beyond the initial 17 days.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jurisdiction_in_reality_and_animation">Jurisdiction in reality and animation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Jurisdiction in reality and animation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The WGA's membership of approximately 12,000 writers (more than 7,000 in WGAW and more than 4,000 in WGAE) primarily work on <a href="/wiki/Live-action" class="mw-redirect" title="Live-action">live-action</a>, script-driven movies and television programs.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Exactly if and how the WGA's Minimum Basic Agreement (MBA) should apply to other TV and film categories such as <a href="/wiki/Reality_television" title="Reality television">reality television</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animation" title="Animation">animation</a> had been inconsistent over the years and were an area of much dispute.</p>
<p>The WGA had been pushing for jurisdiction of reality and animation, but dropped these issues as the WGA and AMPTP entered into informal negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_3">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>The negotiations between the AMPTP and the WGA are at an impasse because the WGA has continued to press a series of unreasonable demands that have nothing to do with new media and the real concerns of most working writers.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">AMPTP statement: <i>The State of the Strike: Day 47</i><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Programs such as <i><a href="/wiki/Real_People" title="Real People">Real People</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/That%27s_Incredible!" title="That's Incredible!">That's Incredible!</a></i>, which were arguably "reality" shows of the 1980s, were covered by the MBA, whereas more recently produced reality shows such as <i><a href="/wiki/Survivor_(U.S._TV_series)" title="Survivor (U.S. TV series)">Survivor</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/America%27s_Next_Top_Model" title="America's Next Top Model">America's Next Top Model</a></i> are not.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Many producers of reality programming argue that since these shows are mostly, if not entirely, unscripted, there is no writer. The WGA counters that the process of creating interesting scenarios, culling raw material, and shaping it into a narrative with conflict, character arc, and storyline constitutes writing and should fall under its contract.</p>
<p>In the summer of 2006, the WGAW attempted to organize employees of <i><a href="/wiki/America%27s_Next_Top_Model" title="America's Next Top Model">America's Next Top Model</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The employees voted to join the WGA, but then they were fired and production continued without them.</p>
<p>Animated films and TV programs have also been an area of heavy contention. The majority of animated film and television writing is not covered by the WGA's MBA. Most animated feature films have been written under the jurisdiction of another union, the <a href="/wiki/International_Alliance_of_Theatrical_Stage_Employees" title="International Alliance of Theatrical Stage Employees">International Alliance of Theatrical Stage Employees</a> Local 839, also known as <a href="/wiki/The_Animation_Guild,_I.A.T.S.E._Local_839" title="The Animation Guild, I.A.T.S.E. Local 839">The Animation Guild</a>. IATSE's jurisdiction stemmed from <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a>'s tradition of creating an animated feature via <a href="/wiki/Storyboards" class="mw-redirect" title="Storyboards">storyboards</a> written and drawn by <a href="/wiki/Storyboard_artist" title="Storyboard artist">storyboard artists</a>. In the years up until the strike, most studios began hiring screenwriters to write script pages which are then storyboarded. According to the WGA, 100% of animated feature film screenplays in 2005 were written by at least one WGA member.<sup id="cite_ref-wgaproposal_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wgaproposal-22">[22]</a></sup> Some animated features, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf_(2007_film)" title="Beowulf (2007 film)">Beowulf</a></i>, were written under the WGA contract.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The only animated television programs affected by the strike were <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Simpsons" title="The Simpsons">The Simpsons</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Family_Guy" title="Family Guy">Family Guy</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Hill" title="King of the Hill">King of the Hill</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/American_Dad!" title="American Dad!">American Dad!</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-TAGposition_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAGposition-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The WGA and the IATSE have an ongoing disagreement as to which union should represent animation writers.<sup id="cite_ref-bitchslapping_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bitchslapping-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposals_2">Proposals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Proposals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Regarding reality programming, the WGA requested contract language clarifying that reality programming does fall under its jurisdiction. They further proposed the adoption of a credit, "Story Producer" and "Supervising Story Producer" to be given to those writers performing story contributions to a reality show.<sup id="cite_ref-wgaproposal-doc_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wgaproposal-doc-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>As for animation, the WGA proposed clarifying its jurisdiction to cover all animation in TV and film that did not encroach on the jurisdiction of another union.<sup id="cite_ref-wgaproposal-doc_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wgaproposal-doc-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conclusion_3">Conclusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Conclusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>WGA president Patric Verrone announced that the reality and animation jurisdiction proposals were formally removed from the table<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Negotiations_and_strike_activity">Negotiations and strike activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Negotiations and strike activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The final negotiations between the WGA and AMPTP before the WGA's contracts expired on October 31, 2007<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> began on October 25, but the talks broke down due to the issues surrounding new-media royalties. After the contracts expired, the WGAW held a meeting at the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Convention_Center" title="Los Angeles Convention Center">Los Angeles Convention Center</a>, which was attended by 3,000 WGAW members, and the negotiating committee formally recommended a strike,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> after which the WGAE and the WGAW officially announced that the strike would begin at 12:01&#160;AM on November 5.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vid_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vid-51">[51]</a></sup> In a last-ditch conciliation to try to avoid the strike, the WGA temporarily withdrew its DVD proposal on November 4, but the companies still insisted on a lack of residual for new media,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and the talks subsequently broke down, with both sides accusing the other of walking out.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Thus, on November 5, nearly 3,000 WGAW members, plus additional <a href="/wiki/Screen_Actors_Guild" title="Screen Actors Guild">SAG</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teamsters" class="mw-redirect" title="Teamsters">Teamsters</a> members, picketed or refused to cross the picket lines at 14 targeted studios in Los Angeles,<sup id="cite_ref-dvd_proposal_removal_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dvd_proposal_removal-23">[23]</a></sup> and many more Writers Guild of America East picketers marched in locations in New York including <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Center" title="Rockefeller Center">Rockefeller Center</a>. The picket lines continued, along with various rallies, throughout the strike period in both cities.</p>
<p>Following four days of targeted picketing, a large rally was held outside the <a href="/wiki/Twentieth_Century_Fox" class="mw-redirect" title="Twentieth Century Fox">Fox studios</a> in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> on Friday, November 9, drawing an estimated 4,000 WGAW members and supporters, including a sizeable number of <a href="/wiki/Screen_Actors_Guild" title="Screen Actors Guild">SAG</a> members.<sup id="cite_ref-rally_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rally-54">[54]</a></sup> Speakers included WGA West president <a href="/wiki/Patric_M._Verrone" class="mw-redirect" title="Patric M. Verrone">Patric M. Verrone</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Family_Guy" title="Family Guy">Family Guy</a></i>/<i><a href="/wiki/American_Dad!" title="American Dad!">American Dad!</a></i> creator <a href="/wiki/Seth_MacFarlane" title="Seth MacFarlane">Seth MacFarlane</a>, civil rights activist <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Jackson" title="Jesse Jackson">Jesse Jackson</a>, and producer <a href="/wiki/Norman_Lear" title="Norman Lear">Norman Lear</a>. The rally was opened with a two-song performance by <a href="/wiki/Zack_de_la_Rocha" title="Zack de la Rocha">Zack de la Rocha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Morello" title="Tom Morello">Tom Morello</a> (as <a href="/wiki/The_Nightwatchman" title="The Nightwatchman">The Nightwatchman</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Rage_Against_the_Machine" title="Rage Against the Machine">Rage Against the Machine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-youtube0104_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-youtube0104-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 16, 2007, both the WGA and the AMPTP made the following announcement: "Leaders from the WGA and the AMPTP have mutually agreed to resume formal negotiations on November 26. No other details or press statements will be issued."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The AMPTP then submitted a new proposal to the WGA on November 29, 2007, reportedly worth an additional $130 million in compensation. The WGA responded that it did not understand how the $130 million figure had been calculated, but was pleased the AMPTP was proposing figures in that range. Both sides agreed to a four-day recess at the WGA's request. Talks were resumed on December 4.</p>
<p>Following a renewed push on jurisdiction in reality programming by the WGA, including a rally outside North American headquarters of non-signatory reality producer <a href="/wiki/FremantleMedia" title="FremantleMedia">FremantleMedia</a>, talks again broke off on December 7 when the AMPTP walked away from the table, issuing a press release that they would return only upon the WGA dropping several key proposals, among them the reality proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> This move had been predicted by reporter <a href="/wiki/Nikki_Finke" title="Nikki Finke">Nikki Finke</a> and others, many of whom suspected the AMPTP was intentionally delaying negotiations so that it could terminate unwanted production contracts via <a href="/wiki/Force_majeure" title="Force majeure">force majeure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a>, the impact of accepting all of WGA's proposals was "largely negligible" and "financially small", suggesting that the studios were afraid that reaching a favorable settlement with the writers might "embolden directors and actors in their coming renegotiations."<sup id="cite_ref-wallstreet_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wallstreet-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mid-December, the WGA announced plans to try to negotiate with individual production companies to end the impasse.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The AMPTP and WGA agreed to resume informal talks in an effort to organize formal negotiations on January 19, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> This was accepted and both parties decided to go back to the negotiating table as of that date, however the president of the WGA had ordered a media black-out, with no WGA employees reporting any news to the media. WGA President Patric Verrone did, however, report on January 22 that the <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike#Jurisdiction_in_reality_and_animation" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 Writers Guild of America strike">animation and reality jurisdiction proposals</a> had been dropped.</p>
<p>On January 25, it was announced that the WGA had made an interim agreement with <a href="/wiki/Lions_Gate" class="mw-redirect" title="Lions Gate">Lions Gate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Studios" title="Marvel Studios">Marvel Studios</a>. However, the AMPTP has commented on these types of agreements as "meaningless", although talks between them and WGA continued and many critics believed the strike could be over within two weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-strikemightbeoverintwoweeks_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strikemightbeoverintwoweeks-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>On February 2, 2008, despite a <a href="/wiki/Media_blackout" title="Media blackout">media blackout</a> agreed to by both parties during unofficial negotiations, multiple media reports suggested there had been significant progress involving breakthroughs on key issues in the talks. Further reporting suggested that by the end of the business week starting February 4, 2008, a contract proposal might be announced.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Pro-WGA blog UnitedHollywood.com confirmed that "creative solutions to the biggest differences between the AMPTP and the WGA have gotten the tentative and cautious approval of both sides", but cautions that the specific legal language has not yet been drafted.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The next day, rumors continued to mount; Deadlinehollywooddaily.com reported that <a href="/wiki/Peter_Chernin" title="Peter Chernin">Peter Chernin</a> had told fellow <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XLII" title="Super Bowl XLII">Super Bowl XLII</a> attendees that "the strike is over."<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>This is not over. Nor is it close. Until the moment it is over, it can never be close. Because if we see the finish line we will flag and they are absolutely counting on us to do that. In the room, reason. On the streets, on the net, I say reason is for the 'moderates'. Remember what they've done. Remember what they're trying to take from us. FIGHT. FIGHT. FIGHT.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><a href="/wiki/Joss_Whedon" title="Joss Whedon">Joss Whedon</a><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>On February 5, 2008, the WGA leadership scheduled a meeting for active members on Saturday, February 9, 2008 to discuss and gain feedback on a proposed contract.<sup id="cite_ref-saturdaymeeting_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-saturdaymeeting-69">[69]</a></sup> TV executives had described the deadline as February 15 for new material to be produced for the 2007–08 television season.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> On February 9, 2008, WGA President Patric Verrone emailed the membership announcing that the WGA leadership and AMPTP had reached a tentative deal. The tentative contract proposals were provided to the membership, and a meeting to discuss them as well as future process was scheduled the same day on both coasts.</p>
<p>According to reports, the first deal discussion meeting for WGAE's members, ended on an optimistic note.<sup id="cite_ref-gothammeetinggoeswell_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gothammeetinggoeswell-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The WGA started a 48-hour vote for guild members on February 10, 2008 regarding a motion on ending the three-month-old strike.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Voting ended for <a href="/wiki/Writers_Guild_of_America,_East" title="Writers Guild of America, East">WGAE</a> at 7PM <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">EST</a>, on February 12, 2008. The <a href="/wiki/Writers_Guild_of_America,_West" title="Writers Guild of America, West">WGAW</a> voted from 2:00 to 6:00&#160;p.m. PST, and at approximately 6:51 PST, WGA president Patric Verone announced that 92.5% of the membership voted to end the strike. On February 26, about 93.6% of WGA members approved a new three-year contract that would be effective until May 1, 2011, with pay hikes ranging from 3 to 3.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the beginning of April 2008, about one and a half months after the end of the strike, the <a href="/wiki/Writers_Guild_of_America,_East" title="Writers Guild of America, East">Writers Guild of America, East</a> filed lawsuit against the <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> television network and Corday Productions over alleged violations of a strike-termination agreement. The legal basis for the suit was that ABC and Corday continued using strike-replacement writers for the soap operas <i><a href="/wiki/All_My_Children" title="All My Children">All My Children</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Days_of_Our_Lives" title="Days of Our Lives">Days of Our Lives</a></i> rather than allowing the original writers to return to work after the end of the strike.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Strategies_and_tactics">Strategies and tactics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Strategies and tactics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="AMPTP">AMPTP</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: AMPTP">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>The WGA organizers are grasping for straws and have never had a coherent strategy for engaging in serious negotiations. The AMPTP may have different companies with different assets in different businesses, but they are all unified in one common goal – to reach an agreement with writers that positions everyone in our industry for success in a rapidly changing marketplace.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">AMPTP spokesman Jesse Hiestand<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Foreseeing the possibility of a strike, production companies accelerated production of films and television episodes in an effort to stockpile enough material to continue regular film releases and TV schedules during the strike period.<sup id="cite_ref-strikezone_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strikezone-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> A list of 300 high-priority film projects reportedly circulated around talent agencies in accordance with this effort.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the refusal of many <a href="/wiki/Showrunners" class="mw-redirect" title="Showrunners">showrunners</a> (writer-producers) to cross the picket line in the first week of the strike, production companies sent breach-of-contract letters and suspended many of them without pay.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>There was also speculation the companies were seeking out other sources of writing services, including in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Writers%27_Guild_of_Great_Britain" title="Writers' Guild of Great Britain">Writers' Guild of Great Britain</a> attempted to thwart this effort, however, by discouraging British union members from participating.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paul_Cornell" title="Paul Cornell">Paul Cornell</a>, a writer for several successful television series in the UK, mentioned in a post on his blog on December 14, 2007, that he had declined an approach to cross the WGA picket line and write for an American series.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Motion_Picture_and_Television_Producers" title="Alliance of Motion Picture and Television Producers">AMPTP</a> announced on December 6 that it had hired the <a href="/wiki/Public_relations" title="Public relations">public relations</a> services of Chris Lehane and Mark Fabiani, self-dubbed the "Masters of Disaster", who had previously worked for Democratic politicians (including <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gray_Davis" title="Gray Davis">Gray Davis</a>) and who, according to the <i><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Chronicle" title="San Francisco Chronicle">San Francisco Chronicle</a></i>, "earn up to $100,000 a month for pulling their clients out of public relations quicksand."<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The AMPTP also hired former <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">Arnold Schwarzenegger</a> campaign manager Steve Schmidt of Mercury Public Affairs in Sacramento.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fabiani &amp; Lehane's strategy appeared to be to try to weaken the WGA membership's resolve and foment resentment and doubt regarding WGA leadership within its ranks and in the film industry at large, especially with <a href="/wiki/Below_the_line_(film_production)" class="mw-redirect" title="Below the line (film production)">below-the-line</a> workers, by framing the strike as "havoc... wreaked... by the WGA's actions" (paraphrased) and by blaming the WGA for "start[ing] this strike".<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> They also appeared to be attempting to recast language in terms more favorable to the AMPTP, such as referring to WGA negotiators as "organizers" and branding the AMPTP proposals as a "New Economic Partnership".<sup id="cite_ref-dhd1211_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhd1211-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response to their work for the AMPTP, Fabiani &amp; Lehane's union clients <a href="/wiki/SEIU" class="mw-redirect" title="SEIU">SEIU</a> Local 99 and <a href="/wiki/Change_To_Win" class="mw-redirect" title="Change To Win">Change To Win</a> terminated their contracts with the consultants.<sup id="cite_ref-dhd1211_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhd1211-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="WGA">WGA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: WGA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>If they gave us everything we had on the table right now, if they gave us everything we wanted—everything—and they then made a deal with the <a href="/wiki/Directors_Guild_of_America" title="Directors Guild of America">DGA</a> and matched it, which is what they'll do, and then they made a deal with the <a href="/wiki/Screen_Actors_Guild" title="Screen Actors Guild">Screen Actors Guild</a> and tripled it, which is typically what happens…if they did that—if they gave us everything—on a company-by-company basis they would be giving all of us less than each of their CEOs makes in a year. And in some cases, a lot less.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">WGAW president Patric Verrone<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>It was initially expected that the strike, if it occurred, would be scheduled for the summer of 2008 to coincide with the expiration of the <a href="/wiki/Screen_Actors_Guild" title="Screen Actors Guild">Screen Actors Guild</a>'s contracts. Instead, the strike started shortly after the WGA's contracts expired. This was apparently done to give the AMPTP less time to stockpile scripts and otherwise prepare for a strike in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the pre-strike negotiations, the WGA created "contract captains" in order to keep the general membership informed on a person-to-person basis of the latest developments. Once the strike started, these members became "strike captains," tasked with communication duties as well as helping to coordinate pickets.</p>
<p>The WGA assigned picketers to location shoots in an attempt to shut down production, and set up picket lines in front of studio gates to encourage <a href="/wiki/Teamsters" class="mw-redirect" title="Teamsters">Teamsters</a>, particularly truck drivers, not to cross the line.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>For its second week of picketing, the WGA reduced their studio strike list from fourteen to ten, shifted picketing hours to earlier in the day, and scheduled a series of daily strike themes ranging from "Bring-A-Star-To-Picket-With-You" (also called "Cast Day") to "Bring-Your-Kids" special events.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>The WGA made a direct appeal to the public to explain the issues behind the strike, including use of online videos and blogs.<sup id="cite_ref-youtube0104_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-youtube0104-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> WGA strike captains also encouraged fans to mail pencils to the film and TV moguls en masse.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> They also considered unorthodox methods, including performing a mock <a href="/wiki/Exorcism" title="Exorcism">exorcism</a> against <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> and holding the <a href="/wiki/Last_rites" title="Last rites">last rites</a> for the former MBA.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, the WGA appealed to members of crew and industry craft unions, including the <a href="/wiki/Teamsters" class="mw-redirect" title="Teamsters">Teamsters</a>, and <a href="/wiki/IATSE" class="mw-redirect" title="IATSE">IATSE</a>, some of whom may not have been aware that their union also received residuals to pay for health and pension programs, and that they were expected to directly benefit from residual gains made by the WGA.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late December, the WGA announced a new "divide and conquer" strategy designed to break the <a href="/wiki/One_Big_Union_(concept)" title="One Big Union (concept)">solidarity</a> of the AMPTP by negotiating strategic interim deals with individual networks, studios, and production companies who were willing to agree to the WGA's proposals. This was intended to put pressure on the other member companies, especially those who were competing with companies that were then able to return to production. The approach resulted in deals with David Letterman's television production company <a href="/wiki/Worldwide_Pants_Incorporated" class="mw-redirect" title="Worldwide Pants Incorporated">Worldwide Pants</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> another with feature studio <a href="/wiki/United_Artists" title="United Artists">United Artists</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> and a third one with film studio <a href="/wiki/The_Weinstein_Company" title="The Weinstein Company">The Weinstein Company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The new strategy contained some risk for the WGA, however, as there was a risk that some members may resent a few writers working while others were still on strike.</p>
<p>On January 14, 2008, two additional side deals were announced by the WGA – one with <a href="/w/index.php?title=Media_Rights_Capital&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Media Rights Capital (page does not exist)">Media Rights Capital</a>, a production company working on both features and television, and the other with <a href="/wiki/Spyglass_Entertainment" title="Spyglass Entertainment">Spyglass Entertainment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> On January 25, 2008, another side deal was reached; the WGA and <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Studios" title="Marvel Studios">Marvel Studios</a> signed an interim comprehensive agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>On February 3, 2008, the WGA made a deal with four more filmmakers in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_television">Effect on television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Effect on television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effect_of_the_2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike_on_television" title="Effect of the 2007–08 Writers Guild of America strike on television">Effect of the 2007–08 Writers Guild of America strike on television</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_employees">Production employees</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Production employees">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because production ceased for all scripted television programming, hundreds or thousands of support staff were laid off by the studios. The AMPTP estimated that WGA writers and crewmembers in the IATSE union lost $342.8 million in wages.<sup id="cite_ref-varietytoll_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-varietytoll-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dale Alexander, the <a href="/wiki/Key_grip" title="Key grip">key grip</a> for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Office_(U.S._TV_series)" title="The Office (U.S. TV series)">The Office</a>,</i> wrote to the <i>LA Times</i>, expressing thoughts on the strike from the perspective of a production employee.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shows">Shows</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_Writers_Guild_of_America_strike&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Shows">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All scripted Hollywood shows except <i><a href="/wiki/October_Road_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="October Road (TV series)">October Road</a></i> were expected to shut down by the week of December 19, 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Hollywood journalist <a href="/wiki/Nikki_Finke" title="Nikki Finke">Nikki Finke</a> reported, "CEOs are determined to write off not just the rest of this TV season (including the Back 9 of scripted series), but also pilot season and the 2008/2009 schedule as well. Indeed, network orders for reality TV shows are pouring into the agencies right now."<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within the first week of the strike, AMPTP-member companies fired writers' assistants, production assistants, and other lower-level staffers working on shut down programs. Writer-producer <a href="/wiki/Seth_MacFarlane" title="Seth MacFarlane">Seth MacFarlane</a> called this a "desperate, punitive act" and called on "all show-runners whose assistants are terminated, if you have the means, keep paying your assistants because this strike is about the little guys."<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Of the "Big Four" networks—<a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a>, <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">FOX</a>—NBC had the most severe ad shortfall as its prime time ratings declined sharply; none of its new shows achieved breakout success.<sup id="cite_ref-mw_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mw-113">[113]</a></sup> Moreover, during 2007, NBC saw its prime time 18-to-49-year-old viewership drop by 11%. CBS dropped the same demographic by 10%, and ABC lost 5%.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Fox executive <a href="/wiki/Peter_Chernin" title="Peter Chernin">Peter Chernin</a> suggested the strike is "probably a positive" for the network, as he expected its non-WGA reality hit <i><a href="/wiki/American_Idol" title="American Idol">American Idol</a></i> to do especially well given reduced competition.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although both are WGA members who pledged support for the writers, <a href="/wiki/Jay_Leno" title="Jay Leno">Jay Leno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conan_O%27Brien" title="Conan O'Brien">Conan O'Brien</a> announced that following the collapse of negotiations, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tonight_Show_with_Jay_Leno" title="The Tonight Show with Jay Leno">The Tonight Show with Jay Leno</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Late_Night_with_Conan_O%27Brien" title="Late Night with Conan O'Brien">Late Night with Conan O'Brien</a></i> would return to air on January 2, 2008, without writers, citing their non-writing staff facing layoffs as the main reason.<sup id="cite_ref-lenobrien_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lenobrien-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-threat_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-threat-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike Leno and O'Brien, whose talk shows were produced and owned by NBC, <a href="/wiki/David_Letterman" title="David Letterman">David Letterman</a> owned his own independent production company, <a href="/wiki/Worldwide_Pants_Incorporated" class="mw-redirect" title="Worldwide Pants Incorporated">Worldwide Pants</a>, which on December 28 announced an "interim agreement" with the WGA. This agreement allowed his talk show and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Late_Late_Show_with_Craig_Ferguson" title="The Late Late Show with Craig Ferguson">The Late Late Show with Craig Ferguson</a></i> to return to air with writers during the strike under terms contained in the WGA's previously-rejected proposals to the AMPTP.</p>
<p>The guild stated it had no plans to target Leno and O'Brien with protests<sup id="cite_ref-dalyprotests_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dalyprotests-118">[118]</a></sup> such as were aimed at non-WGA member <a href="/wiki/Carson_Daly" title="Carson Daly">Carson Daly</a>, who was accused of setting up a joke hotline as a strike-breaking effort<sup id="cite_ref-smokingun_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smokingun-119">[119]</a></sup> when he returned to air.<sup id="cite_ref-withsupport_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-withsupport-120">[120]</a></sup> After being back on air, however, Leno was charged by WGA of strike violation after he penned and delivered monologues, but it is unclear as to what action the guild would take.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lenoreuters_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lenoreuters-122">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lenoline_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenoline-123">[123]</a></sup> Later, <a href="/wiki/Jon_Stewart" title="Jon Stewart">Jon Stewart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Colbert" title="Stephen Colbert">Stephen Colbert</a> announced that their respective shows, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Show" title="The Daily Show">The Daily Show</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Colbert_Report" title="The Colbert Report">The Colbert Report</a></i>, would also return without writers on January 7, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> The WGA accused Comedy Central and NBC of forcing hosts back on air by threatening the jobs of the staff and crew of their shows, and said it would picket them.<sup id="cite_ref-lenobrien_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lenobrien-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-withsupport_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-withsupport-120">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbcdscolb_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcdscolb-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> To show respect to the writers, <i>The Daily Show</i> was renamed, for the duration of the strike, <i><b>A</b></i> <i>Daily Show with Jon Stewart</i>. Similarly, <i>The Colbert Report</i> was rebranded as The Col<i><b>bert</b></i> Re<i><b>port</b></i> (with hard T's) for its first new episode since the strike began. In support of the strike, <a href="/wiki/Screen_Actors_Guild" title="Screen Actors Guild">Screen Actors Guild</a> urged its members to appear on programs that had independent agreements with the WGA, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Late_Show_with_David_Letterman" title="Late Show with David Letterman">Late Show with David Letterman</a></i> and <i>The Late Late Show with Craig Ferguson</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-SAGAlan_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SAGAlan-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>After returning to air, <i>The Daily Show</i> and <i>The Colbert Report</i> experienced an increase in ratings. At the end of January 2008, <i>The Daily Show</i> was up 17% for viewers between 18-34 from January 2007 and up 9% for 18- to 49-year-olds in the same period. <i>The Colbert Report</i> was up 21% for 18- to 34-year-olds and 15% for 18- to 49-year-olds over the same time period. By contrast, <i>The Tonight Show with Jay Leno</i>, <i>Late Night with Conan O'Brien</i>, <i>Jimmy Kimmel Live!</i> and <i>The Late Late Show with Craig Ferguson</i> experienced a fall in ratings against the previous year, while the <i>Late Show with David Letterman</i> remained level.</p>
